```

"Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message
news:5le2dsF7sjk6U1@mid.individual.net...
[snip]
> My point was that unqualified plural nouns have no place in debate or
> argument. I stand by it.
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> that the issue is more about straight and crooked thinking than it is
about
> semantics.

Then, perhaps, your objection should be "that [unquantified]
plural nouns ...". While a quantifier is a qualifier, "quantifier"
has a particular meaning with regard to logic--a meaning that
relates to the use of "all", "some", or the like, in expressions. <g>

I have remained on the periphery of these discussions, in part,
because I can not handle the imprecision; but not in the way
to which you object. That is, I can accept that unquantified
plural nouns may admit of some exceptions. However, I have
a problem with "index versus subscript" precisely because an
index-name, in the context being argued, is a subscript; the
alternative to index-name being identifier. Here, I rely on the
COBOL standard to determine precise usage and do not rely
on common usage.
```

- **References:** `<75vFi.2942$3Y1.159@newssvr17.news.prodigy.net> <5le2dsF7sjk6U1@mid.individual.net> <fctu4t$c7i$1@reader1.panix.com> <5lfe72F7vmg1U1@mid.individual.net>`

```
In article <5lfe72F7vmg1U1@mid.individual.net>,
Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
>
>
…[22 more quoted lines elided]…
>Please clarify in crayon, without joined-up writing.

Righto... assume that your assertion 'unqualified plural nouns have no 
place in debate or argument' was made in the course of a debate or 
argument.

Now... consider that there is a noun, call it (noun), which is defined as 
'an unqualified plural noun'.

Your sentence then becomes '(x) have no place in debate or argument' and 
contradicts it'sself because (x) is, by definition, an unqualified plural 
noun.

See how easy?

DD
```

```
On Thu, 20 Sep 2007 09:39:09 +0000 (UTC), docdwarf@panix.com () wrote:

>I don't recall that being taught in any of the college or university 
>classes I attended, Mr Wagner... but a professor at a college I attended, 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>particular kind of degree.

Many times an organization has changed its name from "college" to
"university", not because one word was more accurate - but because it
was what the big institutions were called.   (A modern American
university's colleges aren't like Oxford's historical colleges, they
are just departments).

When Asimov's Foundation series was re-released late in his life, The
Good Doctor resisted the temptation to write it better.   But one
thing he changed was the word "atomic" to "nuclear".     It really
isn't any more meaningful to say "nuclear energy" - except that our
language changed since he first wrote the books.
```

Integral to the undergraduate college are three graduate schools: the 
Dartmouth Medical School (established 1797), Thayer School of Engineering 
(1867)�which also serves as the undergraduate department of engineering 
sciences�and Tuck School of Business (1900). With these graduate programs, 
conventional American usage would accord Dartmouth the label of "Dartmouth 
University";[6] however, because of historical and nostalgic reasons (such 
as Dartmouth College v. Woodward), the school uses the name "Dartmouth 
College" for the entire institution.[7]

As a result, in the summer of 1816, the New Hampshire Legislature under 
the leadership of the new governor, William Plummer, passed on a strictly 
partisan vote an act which changed the makeup of the Board of Trustees of 
Dartmouth College, enlarging it from twelve to 21, instituting a Board of 
Overseers of 25 to be appointed by the governor and to have veto powers 
over the Trustees, and establishing not Dartmouth College but Dartmouth 
University.

--end quoted text

... but they got rid of the University with some fancy pleading to the

```
docdwarf@panix.com wrote:
> In article <rkt4f3562cuevuau0d8hont92qj8jc564o@4ax.com>,
> Howard Brazee  <howard@brazee.net> wrote:
…[22 more quoted lines elided]…
> 
Interesting.  I was taught a University is a collection of colleges.

Here in Ontario, a "college" is either a post secondary institution that 
grants diplomas but not degrees, while a University is a collection of 
colleges that generally grant the actual degree.  For example, Trinity 
College is part of the University of Toronto or Ryerson College, which 
is a unaffiliated technical school granting various diplomas only. When 
you attend Trinity College, you get a degree from U.ofT, not Trinity. My 
University(Western), has nine colleges.

Stateside, I understand that the equivalent usage is usually geographic 
... UCLA at Berkley, for example, though I am not nearly as familiar 
with schools stateside. I believe that England uses a system similar to 
Canada ... perhaps someone there could comment.

Donald
```

On 20 Sep, 17:54, donald tees <donaldt...@execulink.com> wrote:

> > In article <rkt4f3562cuevuau0d8hont92qj8jc5...@4ax.com>,

In the UK, the College may be a Sixth-form College where the last
sixth and seventh year of secondary education is taught (one can leave
school at the fifth year). At Oxford, Cambridge and Wales, colleges
are distinct administrative units which award degrees from the
University (and Ph.Ds). My degree came from the Universitas Cambrensis
(U of Wales) ex collegio Swansea (from University College of
Swansea).

There is no apparent relationship between the size of the
establishment and whether it is a college or an university.

To muddy matters further, UC Swansea is now renaming itself the
University of Swansea. There is more prestige in being a University
(outside of the Oxbridge groups) than a College.
```

```
Howard Brazee wrote:
> On Thu, 20 Sep 2007 09:39:09 +0000 (UTC), docdwarf@panix.com () wrote:
> 
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> are just departments).

Here in Ontario, they are more than simple departments.  They usually 
are financially independent(often church affiliated), and often have 
their own unique facilities, such as residences. They *always* have 
their own buildings and campuses. They would also have their own staff, 
students, rules, etc.  In effect, they are completely independent 
schools with an affiliation to other colleges, making up a single 
University.

There are also independent colleges that do not have an affiliation with 
a higher institution.  They can grant any kind of diploma they wish, 
though not a degree. They range from respectable technical training to 
"anything for a buck" granting diplomas in pottery and basket weaving.

> 
> When Asimov's Foundation series was re-released late in his life, The
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> language changed since he first wrote the books.

Donald

Speaking of oddities in the language related to universities, I got my
masters at the University of Denver (D.U.), and work at The University
of Colorado (C.U.).     

There are a bunch of ambiguous university acronyms though.   Around
here U.N.C. is University of Northern Colorado, not University of
North Carolina.   Colorado College has more than hockey, it also has
graduate programs.

When I was in high school, I lived in Connecticut and heard someone
say he was going to U-Conn, and thought he meant that territory next
to Alaska.

I'd rather go to "Apple University" than to the "College of
Cardinals".

```
On Thu, 13 Sep 2007 22:11:40 +1200, "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
wrote:

>
>
…[55 more quoted lines elided]…
>I remember this from a philosophical logic class when a very young man.

There are two kinds of logic: deductive and inductive. What you say is true under
deductive, it is not true under inductive. 

Inductive logic deals with generalities. For example, under inductive logic 'grains are
nutritious' means MOST grains. Under deductive logic, the statement would be rejected
because the speaker had not examined every kernel of grain ... past, present and future. 
The phrase 'grains' is called an unenumerated class. Under deductive, it includes every
member of the class; under inductive, it means the vast majority. 

Statistics, a branch of inductive, quantifies the degree of certainty with Confidence
Interval (CI). When the CI is unstated, the default is 95%. Thus, when a scientist says
grains are nutritions, he means 95%, not every kernel.

Inductive generalities are how humans survived through evolution. It is also known as
Common Sense. A person who insisted on certainty before eating grains would starve to
death waiting for the results. David Hume was an example. He argued that inductive is an
invalid form of logic. Your teacher was another. Such people are called pendants, which is
defined in the dictionary as "lacking common sense".  Now you know what that means.
```

>>>>>> CLC regulars are more interested in recreational pedantry than they 
>>>>>> are
…[60 more quoted lines elided]…
>Nice try. :-)

I offered a serious explanation. The difference is not American usage nor military
background, it's between two approaches to logic -- applied versus pure. Most people,
including scientists and engineers, use the applied method; logicians take a pure
approach.

Communication requires that the sender and receiver share the same grammatical premises.  
When they differ, the resulting semantic misunderstandings are the most common source of
mistrust and hostility. For example:

You said X(2).
No I didn't, I meant X(1). 
Don't try to change your story, liar. I heard you say X.
You didn't understand. X means Y(1). 
No it doesn't, it means Y(2).
Oh yeah, take your X and shove it.
Who's gonna make me?
```

exception and is a sweeping statement if so much as one donkey can be
proven to have never eaten proferred carrots but to say "Donkeys
eat...." may mean one, some or all donkeys eat carrots and not "all

```


"Robert" <no@e.mail> wrote in message 
news:au8ue3pvjavprb75ggi2op6mdp0l55kq6t@4ax.com...
> On Mon, 17 Sep 2007 09:21:24 -0600, Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net> 
> wrote:
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
> calculating payroll and formatting reports.

For many (most?) that is precisely the case.  And these "mundane tasks" 
should not be dismissed so lightly. They can be challenging and stimulating, 
and certainly bring their own classes of problems to be solved.
>
> The tragedy is that they've given up, lost their aspiration/dream. They're 
> just putting in
> the hours and collecting the paycheck. They've become cyber whores.

Some of us have ALWAYS (well for thirty years, anyway) been cyber whores :-)

(I don't see that term as derogatory, provided it is of  the person 
concerned's free will...but I would only apply it to contractors; permanent 
staff don't have such obnoxious pimps... :-) )

And there is a time to let something go.

While I agree that the loss of dreams and aspirations is a sad thing, and to 
be avoided if possible, sometimes you simply need a better dream.

COBOL served the world well for half a century; what is the point in simply 
crying over the Glory Days?

It isn't a relevant paradigm for the twenty first century. Sooner or later 
it was going to go; the amazing thing is that it lasted as long as it did. 
(PL/I died 10 years ago and nobody noticed ... :-))

The world moved on. COBOL people (unless they are heading towards retirement 
anyway and it is too late to start a new career) should do likewise.

It ISN'T tragic, it is just reality.

Pete.
```

It depends on their mission statement. I would agree if it says:

The Acme IT organization takes pride in resisting change since our
CIO wrote his last line of code in 1980. In addition, out most complex
programs can be understood by the least knowledgeable on our staff
-- the production support person. That's essential because we 
have LOTS of production support issues.  

We believe in the KISS approach to error handling. An an Acme programmer
in training, you will be on call one week out of five. Prepare for it, because you 
will be solving urgent problems (we call them kisses) 16 hours per day. When 
you're chasing a SOC7 at 2am, you will welcome the lack of sophistication 
in our code base.

maintained to death, which can lead to bad code.  And it is a shame
you stayed away from mainframe shops but if you want to limit your
exposure and experience, that's up to you.  Personally, I've stayed
away from anyplace that runs RPG.

> goot enough qualification?

true to write:

CLC regulars are more interested in writing good Cobol than they are
in recreational pedantry.

If, as your assertion about qualification claims, both are equally
true, then the combination is meaningless.

Need I say, as usual.

    people whatever

your statement qualifies and subsets this to:

It is now quite specific about whom you were referring to and that is
the complete set you have twice qualified.

- **References:** `<dldhd39vccjgdgs3g1572hbi2eq2suil7u@4ax.com> <1189392749.625750.86680@y42g2000hsy.googlegroups.com> <4ml9e39bfkd30oa1e0o7tdhs6eef49kdtg@4ax.com> <1189405361.877179.168910@w3g2000hsg.googlegroups.com> <ds0ce3d60gk2m7apm3icuag2io65thtdlt@4ax.com> <75vFi.2942$3Y1.159@newssvr17.news.prodigy.net> <attee3hhoje0r9r6e67f6v1cnrj1jd5j64@4ax.com> <tyQFi.7676$z_5.2320@nlpi069.nbdc.sbc.com>`

```

"Doug Miller" <spambait@milmac.com> schrieb im Newsbeitrag 
news:tyQFi.7676$z_5.2320@nlpi069.nbdc.sbc.com...
> In article <attee3hhoje0r9r6e67f6v1cnrj1jd5j64@4ax.com>, Robert 
> <no@e.mail> wrote:
…[28 more quoted lines elided]…
> than adding 1.


Actually, for P4's, the INC/DEC instructions are performance killers.
Even Intel document that in their optimization manual.
And gcc knows about this -
int test()
{
static int rr = 0;
return ++rr;
}
gcc -O2 -S test.c
Relevant bit of assembler -
        incl    %eax
gcc -O2 -S -mcpu=pentium4 test.c
Relevant bit of assembler -
        addl    $1, %eax

Note recent gcc's use -mtune instead of -mcpu
```

On Wed, 12 Sep 2007 12:01:35 GMT, spambait@milmac.com (Doug Miller) wrote:

>stated.

Not true. Here's the code:

perform varying binary-number from 1 by 1 until binary-number > 10 *> 154
perform varying binary-number from 10 by -1 until binary-number = 0  *> 153
perform varying binary-number from 10 by -1 until binary-number = comp5-number *> 163
perform 10 times   *> 233

The difference between 1, 2 and 3 is 6%, which is too small to merit a recommendation.
The difference between the first three and TIMES in 4 is 34%, which IS signifigant.

They wasted our time with recommendations that don't matter while ignoring the one that
does.

```
On Sep 13, 4:03 pm, Robert <n...@e.mail> wrote:

> >No, you didn't -- because you didn't even test the proposition that you
> >stated.
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> The difference between 1, 2 and 3 is 6%, which is too small to merit a recommendation.

You seem to completely miss the point of the recommendation. By using
literal 10 in each you seem to want to process every occurs rather
than what is normal which is to process the actual data items.
Realistic code would be:

     perform varying x from 1 by 1 until x > this-limit

vs   perform varying x from this-limit by -1 until x = 0

The recommended way _is_ faster.  You claimed with your 'busted' that
it wasn't.

On a machine I have here it was 22% faster, and yes I did use Comp-5.
You still haven't absorbed the idea that different machines will give
different results. Regardless of whether it is 6 or 22 or 100% the
recommendation is not 'busted'.


> The difference between the first three and TIMES in 4 is 34%, which IS signifigant.

Irrelevant to the particular recommendation.

In fact I got perform comp-5 times to be slower by a few percent and
perform literal times to be fastest of all by a few percent.

It seems to only be 'significantly slower' on your hardware.

> They wasted our time with recommendations that don't matter while ignoring the one that
> does.

On my hardware it _does_ matter and TIMES may or may not. The problem
with TIMES is that you don't get to use the variable as an index or
subscript or whatever and this may introduce additional code. But
whatever, literal TIMES is faster on my machine and variable TIMES is
slowest.
```

The most widely distributed and used RPG in the world is the Soviet
Union-developed RPG-7. The design was suggested to the Soviet Union by
Jacques Williams, a French weapon developer. The Soviets developed the
basic design of the RPG during WW II, combining important design
features of the US Bazooka and the German Panzerfaust. Today, advanced
militaries such as that of the United States military, have implanted
armor on their tanks that are invulnerable to grenades. Because of
this, the RPG has become much less effective for guerrilla and
insurgent fighters that have no accessibility or money to buy better
weapons.
</QUOTE>

```
"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote:
> LX-i <lxi0007@netscape.net> wrote:
>
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> invasion a failure.

I don't want to get into the whole "Iraq war was/wasn't a mistake/failure/
disaster, etc." issue. But I would like to point out a couple of points that
I believe are important, and far too many people fail to see.

1. Everybody makes mistakes. There has never been a war, or any human
other endeavor of comparable size, that was conducted without error. No
matter which leaders or political party is in power, they will make mistakes.
Hopefully, the 'better' leaders will make fewer, and less serious, mistakes.
Expecting anyone to run a war without error is irrational, and guaranteed
to produce disappointment and dissatisfaction to no good purpose.

2. A 'perfect' outcome is not always possible, no matter what is done.
It seems that most of the news media and population simply do not
understand this, and it causes them to always be critical of any solution,
even if it might be the best that could be done, under the circumstances.
Sometimes there is not even a 'desirable' outcome, but only one that
is hopefully the 'least undesirable'. Expecting there to always be a 'perfect'
or even a 'good' solution to every problem is also irrational, and guaranteed
to produce disappointment and dissatisfaction to no good purpose.

I believe people in earlier generations understood these things much better
than people do today, mostly because they had to deal with the 'real world'
much more directly than we pampered and protected modern citizens,
with all our modern societal safety nets.
```

Agreed.    Any endeavor needs to be measured primarily against its
goals.    Looking at costs and other benefits is part of the
evaluation as well.     We won't find any endeavor that is mistake
free - but we can still evaluate whether the original goal was
accomplished.

the hands of unfriendly powers then the starting point is "success".
But I don't think anybody is measuring that goal so narrowly.

It's kind of like replacing a data exception with a divide by zero
exception, and saying you achieved your goal.

An endeavor should be judged also against what is reasonably possible.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol Myth Busters

- **From:** Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2007-09-14T14:51:11-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1189806671.116474.287140@50g2000hsm.googlegroups.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<1189803093.741172.21600@y42g2000hsy.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<dldhd39vccjgdgs3g1572hbi2eq2suil7u@4ax.com> <1189392749.625750.86680@y42g2000hsy.googlegroups.com> <1189803093.741172.21600@y42g2000hsy.googlegroups.com>`

```
On Sep 15, 8:51 am, Alistair <alist...@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> wrote:
> On 10 Sep, 03:52, Richard <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:
>
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> of decimal places in the sources and is not arbitrarily assigned.

That is generally correct, though it is required to be 'at least' that
size.  Fujitsu had a bug in one version, 4 I think, where for a
multiply of S9(x)V99 by S9(x)V99 the intermediate result was S9(x
+x)V99 instead of V9999.

A divide, or course, can result in an arbitrary number of decimal
places. The manual I have says: "the expression is evaluated to as
many digits as possible". The default for MF is ARITHMETIC(MF) of 18
digits before the point and 18 after.

But regardless of that the intermediate result will generally require
larger operands than the individual statements where the programmer
has direct control over the size of the results he needs. This will
usually result in slower to much slower operation. But then trivial
stuff such as simple assignment may show no difference (because there
is no intermediate result).

Robert showed that he didn't understand the advice and was only
interested in denigrating it regardless of whether it was true or not.
He showed this by still concluding "They wasted our time with
recommendations that don't matter while ignoring the one that
does." even after it had been shown that he hadn't 'busted' anything,
even the ones he made up and assigned to 'legacy coders' to denigrate
them.

The whole purpose of his tirade on the advice was, it seems, to
eliminate any source of advice on Cobol that isn't Robert's, thus
making him the sole messiah of 'good cobol'.

In that crusade, 'pedants' are those who don't agree with him.
```

- **References:** `<dldhd39vccjgdgs3g1572hbi2eq2suil7u@4ax.com> <1189392749.625750.86680@y42g2000hsy.googlegroups.com> <1189803093.741172.21600@y42g2000hsy.googlegroups.com>`

```


"Alistair" <alistair@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> wrote in message 
news:1189803093.741172.21600@y42g2000hsy.googlegroups.com...
> On 10 Sep, 03:52, Richard <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:
>> On Sep 1, 2:22 pm, Robert <n...@e.mail> wrote:
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
>
The handling of "intermediate results" (for COMPUTE and other places where 
"arithmetic expressions" are allowed) is explicitly IMPLEMENTER defined in the 
COBOL Standard (until the '02 Standard where by default it is so, but there is 
also a "STANDARD ARITHMETIC" which can be chosen to make results portable).  The 
'02 definitions is planned to be made OBSOLETE in the next Standard (and 
optional) but will be replaced by another optional (never default) well defined 
behavior.

You can look at the Micro Focus "ARITHMETIC" directive to see just a FEW of the 
different ways that these can be handled.

P.S.  My favorite, is the fact that it is fully conforming in the '85 Standard 
to always return 0 or a random number when evaluating an arithmetic expression. 
MF actually came close to this in their ORIGINAL (long corrected) handling of 
fractional exponentiation.
```

Understandably, MF cannot speak to the speed of "your own routine". It would be
interesting to see which of these is faster:

01  SOURCE-NUMBER PIC  9(09)V9999 VALUE .0050.
01  DESTINATION-NUMBER PIC 9(09)V99,

COMPUTE DESTINATION-NUMBER ROUNDED = SOURCE-NUMBER

versus

COMPUTE DESTINATION-NUMBER = SOURCE-NUMBER + .005

My guess is the second will be faster. We'll know tomorrow.

>What if 'source-number' is negative ?

It cannot be negative because it doesn't have a sign. The generalized case is
problematical because Cobol doesn't have a function that returns the sign. I would have to
write 

IF  SOURCE-NUMBER IS NEGATIVE
    COMPUTE DESTINATION-NUMBER = SOURCE-NUMBER - .005
ELSE
    COMPUTE DESTINATION-NUMBER = SOURCE-NUMBER + .005
END-IF
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: What MF says about ROUNDED (was: Cobol Myth Busters

_(reply depth: 5)_

- **From:** Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net>
- **Date:** 2007-09-20T07:58:08-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<vqu4f3h5crle9tvogut75uf1kk3de2bn1i@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<dldhd39vccjgdgs3g1572hbi2eq2suil7u@4ax.com> <cPZHi.10404$TK1.616@fe04.news.easynews.com> <4a01f3tksem1r0vjvinr40ofv6a31cufj6@4ax.com> <1190176509.690345.86390@y27g2000pre.googlegroups.com> <8ni3f3pdbp1t5vt8h05q31siocf6q4h8nj@4ax.com>`

```
On Wed, 19 Sep 2007 20:25:24 -0500, Robert <no@e.mail> wrote:

>IF  SOURCE-NUMBER IS NEGATIVE
>    COMPUTE DESTINATION-NUMBER = SOURCE-NUMBER - .005
>ELSE
>    COMPUTE DESTINATION-NUMBER = SOURCE-NUMBER + .005
>END-IF

If this is necessary, then we should re-evaluate whether it is more
efficient than using ROUNDED.

Functions don't know how they are going to be used, and have to
include such checks, which slows things down.

One thing that I don't like about CoBOL is the way we belt and
suspenders everything:

IF DIVISISOR = ZERO
     MOVE ZERO TO RESULT
ELSE
     DIVIDE DIVIDEND BY DIVISOR GIVING RESULT
END-IF

instead of
     DIVIDE DIVIDEND BY DIVISOR GIVING RESULT
          ON ERROR MOVE ZERO TO RESULT
```

```
>>> On 9/20/2007 at 7:58 AM, in message
<vqu4f3h5crle9tvogut75uf1kk3de2bn1i@4ax.com>, Howard
Brazee<howard@brazee.net> wrote:
> On Wed, 19 Sep 2007 20:25:24 -0500, Robert <no@e.mail> wrote:
> 
…[23 more quoted lines elided]…
>           ON ERROR MOVE ZERO TO RESULT

Are you saying the latter is not allowed or that it's allowed and no one
uses it?
It's not allowed as-is, but this is:

DIVIDE DIVIDEND BY DIVISOR GIVING RESULT
ON SIZE ERROR                              
    MOVE ZERO TO RESULT                 
END-DIVIDE                                 

As is this:

COMPUTE RESULT = DIVIDEND / DIVISOR
ON SIZE ERROR                         
    MOVE ZERO TO TOTAL-LEN            
END-COMPUTE                        

Still kind of a pain in that you have to remember to do it, but I can't
think of a better way.

Frank
```

Common Exception Handling was added in the '02 Standard - but not fully 
implemented by any compiler (as far as I know) and is not "well received" by 
many who have studied it.

For IBM mainframes, LE exception handling is available.  I know of sites using 
it for some things, but I don't personally know of any that use it "generally".

```
"Frank Swarbrick" <Frank.Swarbrick@efirstbank.com> wrote in message 
news:46F4154F.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com...
>>>> On 9/21/2007 at 5:01 PM, in message
<snip>
> From reading the COBOL 2002 standard and later revisions many, many times I
> am still unable to 'grok' the use of 'common exception handling' in COBOL.
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>

One of the "issues" with the '02 Common Exception Handling "model" was the 
decision to use DECLARATIVES - as these were already a "part of the language" 
for a few exceptions.  there is LIMITED control on such things in the '02 
Standard by using the
   >>TURN
compiler directing statement to "localize" where detecting exceptions occurs. 
The RESUME statement (which will probably be made optional in the next 
revision - if any) has some options for how/where execution resumes (if the 
program decides to do so).

I don't know if this helps any - and I certainly think that the basic "desing" 
is part of the reason that no compiler vendor has fully implemented it (and few 
have implemented it at all).  If you care, I believe the model is MOST similar 
to that which has (alwasy?) been available with PL/I (or even the EXEC CICS 
HANDLE CONDITON).  The two designs are NOT identical, but they are more similar 
to each other than to newer Try/Catch/Finally type designs.
```
